return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>1.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.4</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">6.387(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">6.387(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">10.656(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">9.041(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">9.039(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">10.631(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">this work</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Microstructural evaluation of the cathode powder was performed using SEM, which indicated platelet morphology with dense and a submicron cube-like particle shape for primary particles with a smooth surface and sizes in the range of 3–10 μm forming secondary aggregates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d). Overall, the crystallographic and morphological assessments indicate an impurity-free phase without any significant morphological inhomogeneities. Because iron was used as the dopant for the vanadium sites, Mössbauer spectroscopy was employed as an excellent technique to assess the oxidation state and local bonding environment of iron in the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> structure. The room-temperature <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer spectrum of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> material prepared by the hydrothermal method is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> and the corresponding hyperfine parameters are presented in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Mössbauer spectrum recorded at room temperature of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> material prepared by the hydrothermal method.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Hyperfine Parameters of the Room-Temperature Mössbauer Spectra of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> Material</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="center"><col align="center"><col align="center"><col align="center"></colgroup><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="center">iron sites</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">δ&nbsp;(mm&nbsp;s<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">Δ&nbsp;(mm&nbsp;s<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">Γ&nbsp;(mm&nbsp;s<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="center">Fe(III)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">0.32(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">0.42(1)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="center">0.25(1)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The Mössbauer spectrum recorded at room temperature consisted of a symmetric doublet. The spectrum did not exhibit any magnetic splitting, which confirms the paramagnetic behavior of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> at room temperature and is consistent with the reported Mössbauer results in the literature for phosphate-based materials. This behavior also indicates that no magnetic iron-based impurities such as hematite α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> or magnetite Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> were present, which is in good agreement with the XRD results.</div><div class="NLM_p">The symmetric spectrum indicated one iron site in the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> structure similar to the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>1.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.4</sub> reported by Shakhova et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> A good quality fit of the Mössbauer spectrum of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> was obtained using only one doublet attributed to the Fe<sup>3+</sup> component. The obtained values of the isomer shift (0.32 mm s<sup>–1</sup>) and quadrupole splitting (0.42 mm s<sup>–1</sup>) were consistent with the high-spin state of Fe<sup>3+</sup> in FeO<sub>6</sub>-type octahedral sites. This result also confirms that iron was doped in the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical behavior of the vanadium redox couple in the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode material was first investigated using cyclic voltammetry (CV). At different scan rates, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a illustrates the CV curves of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>, which exhibits two active redox couples in the 1.5–4.5 V voltage window. The first redox couple with anodic and cathodic peaks centered at around 3.7 and 3.6 V, respectively, corresponded to the V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> redox couple and are attributed to the reversible extraction and insertion of one sodium atom into the crystal structure of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. The second redox couple with anodic and cathodic peaks at 4.0 and 3.9 V, respectively, corresponded to the vanadium redox and the reversible extraction and insertion of a second sodium atom.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical assessment of the synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode, (a) cyclic voltammograms showing the initial conditioning cycles, (b) cyclic voltammograms at various scan rates from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (c) peak currents with increasing scan rates for the redox couple V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>, and (d) peak currents with increasing scan rates for the redox couple V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">These two redox couples enabled extraction/insertion of two sodium atoms associated with V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> consistent with those reported for Na<sub>3</sub>(VO)<sub>1.7</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.3</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>1.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.4</sub>, and Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref24 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref24 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 19, 24 and 26 to 29">(19,24,26−29)</a> Additionally, the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> material operates at the average voltage of ∼3.85 V, which is higher than those reported for NaFe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>) (2.8 V), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Na<sub>1.86</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> and Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> sodium cathodes. After four conditioning cycles, CV tests were performed at different scan rates from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in a potential range of 2.0–4.5 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). As the scan rate increased, the height and area of the CV curves increased because of the constant capacity of the electrode, which indicates that no side reactions occurred, and a single-phase process occurred during the sodium insertion and extraction at different rates during cycling. The linear relationship between the redox peak current and the square root of the scan rate is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c,d.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> was investigated using the galvanostatic charge/discharge technique. The curve shows that the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/Na half-cell exhibited two potential plateaus at 3.65 and 4.1 V with good Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion processes during cycling. The voltage profiles present the two defined plateaus related to sodium-ion extraction and insertion. The two plateaus observed as shown in the charge/discharge profile and agreed well with the reported charge/discharge behavior of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>1.6</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1.4</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> and Na<sub>3</sub>(VO<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Two larger plateaus at 4.02/4.25 and 3.74/3.96 V, respectively, also corresponded to the redox reactions observed by the CV measurements. The Na half-cell shows good cycling performance with a stable discharge capacity of 98 mA h/g after 100 cycles with a capacity retention of about 85% corresponding to a high Coulombic efficiency of 99.9% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Charge/discharge curves and (b) cycling performance and Coulombic efficiencies at a 1.0 C rate for Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. The cells were assembled in half-cells with Na as the counter and reference electrode and cycled in the voltage window of 2.75–4.5 V with DMC–EC–PC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/Na half-cell was tested using different electrolyte formulations of DMC–EC–PC, DEC–EC, and EC–PC at C/10. The higher discharge capacity was obtained with the DMC–EC–PC electrolyte compared with DMC-free compositions, DEC–EC and EC–PC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), which can be attributed to an improved ionic conductivity and better wettability for DMC-based electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Battery testing at high temperatures is an important criterion for possible commercialization of sodium battery systems. It is also useful in the case of a system deployed under relatively high-temperature conditions (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>). Thus, the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/Na half-cell was cycled at 45 °C using different electrolyte formulations DMC–EC–PC and EC–PC at 1.0 C. The obtained discharge capacity in the half-cell configuration was ∼100 mA h/g for the two different electrolyte formulations. However, high temperatures up to 45 °C may have had a severe effect on the capacity retention, which dropped significantly within 14 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a> shows the performance of the full-cell NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>MWCNT//Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> in 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in an EC–DMC electrolyte cycled between 0.5 and 3.25 V at a 0.2 C rate. The full cell delivered an initial discharge capacity of 80 mA h/g (current density of 24 mA/g) with a moderate Coulombic efficiency of 85% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>). The discharge capacity decreased to 50 mA h/g after 100 cycles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows the evolution of the charge/discharge voltage profiles for selected cycle numbers. The full-cell NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-MWCNT//Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> showed an average discharge voltage of around 1.8 V.</div><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Interfacial Kinetics and Ionic Diffusivity</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a> displays the time–voltage relationship of the cell in the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements at the first cycle. The same measurement was repeated for the second charge/discharge cycle. EIS measurements were performed at each steady state after voltage relaxation. Impedance spectra of selected sodium content (<i>x</i> values) are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b,c</a> for the first and second cycles, respectively. The shape of the impedance spectra changed from two semicircles in the first cycle to one semicircle (overlapping) in the second cycle. The measured impedance spectra (Nyquist plot) of the cell (Na/electrolyte/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>) in the first cycle comprised the following features:<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" id="list1" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">i</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The intercept at the real axis at high frequencies represents the ionic resistance of the electrolyte solution (<i>R</i><sub>1</sub>). It also contains a minor contribution of the solid electrolyte interphase. The inductive effect (<i>L</i><sub>1</sub>) appeared below the real axis at higher frequency which is consistent with the relationship (<i>L</i> ∝ <i>f</i>).</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">ii</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The semicircle at the Na/electrolyte interface appeared at medium–high frequencies because of the expected lower capacitance value. Therefore, the first semicircle is likely due to the charge-transfer resistance at the Na/electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>2</sub>) boundary layer.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">iii</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">On the other hand, a higher capacitance value is expected at the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interface. The observed second semicircle at medium–low frequencies is likely due to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>3</sub>) at the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interface.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">iv.</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Obviously, Warburg response (Ws) appeared at the lower frequency’s interval.</p></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_p">In the second cycle, the charge-transfer resistance at the metallic sodium/electrolyte and Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interfaces was overlapped because of the similar relaxation process and appeared to be a single semicircle. The individual resistance processes were decoupled from the impedance spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b,c</a>) by fitting with the equivalent circuit (given in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) and the evaluated data for the semicircles are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Evolution of different resistances separated from the half-cell configuration Na/electrolyte/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F in the (a) cycle number one and (b) cycle number two. (c) Extracted relaxation time obtained by fitting the depolarization cell voltage as a function of sodium content in Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> and (d) sodium-ion diffusivity.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the corroboration that can help differentiate between the charge-transfer resistance processes at the Na/electrolyte and Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interfaces, the authors calculated the capacitance value from the circuit elements which was relatively lower at the Na/electrolyte interface than at the Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte boundary layer. Details of calculation about capacitance (<i>C</i>) values were discussed in our previous report <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> using the equation <i>C</i> = (<i>R</i><sup>1–<i>n</i></sup><i>Q</i>)<sup>1/<i>n</i></sup>. On an average, error was ∼3.53% between experimental and simulated data. The calculated capacitance values were ∼5 × 10<sup>–5</sup> F for <i>R</i><sub>3</sub> at the Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interface and ∼10<sup>–7</sup> F for <i>R</i><sub>2</sub> at the Na/electrolyte interface. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> displays that the resistance <i>R</i><sub>3</sub> gradually decreased with increasing SOC; on the other hand, <i>R</i><sub>1</sub> remained almost constant as a function of SOC. It is worth noting that the values of interfacial resistances reduced in the second charging process compared to the first charging/discharge cycle. The charge-transfer resistance decreased gradually with the removal of sodium from the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. The significant amount of capacity loss was observed in the first charge/discharge cycle which was consistent with the measured resistances in the first and second cycles (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b). The reason for such a change is not clearly apprehended at this moment. It is expected that the particle deagglomeration might happen and more surface area was exposed to the electrolyte solution. Nonetheless, the cell performance is mainly limited by the interfacial charge-transfer process. Note that no valent change has happened at the Na/electrolyte interface in the SOC and discharge processes and <i>R</i><sub>2</sub> values should not be changed with the SOC or state of discharge (SOD). However, change in electronic conductivity of the active particle resulted from the partial desodiation might be associated with <i>R</i><sub>2</sub> because of the similar relaxation time constant. The pattern of the <i>R</i><sub>2</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>3</sub> was almost similar (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b) as a function of sodium content; however, the magnitude of <i>R</i><sub>2</sub> was much lower than that of <i>R</i><sub>3</sub>. Here, <i>R</i><sub>3</sub> was mainly associated with the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/liquid electrolyte interface which was impacted by the formation of mixed valence V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>) in the course of the charge/discharge cycle. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> demonstrates that the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interface was highly resistive and most likely the cell performances were limited by the charge-transfer process at this interface. The electrode microstructure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows that particles were agglomerated which might be the reason for the huge value of <i>R</i><sub>3</sub> as explained above.</div><div class="NLM_p">During the GITT measurement, sodium was depleted gradually from the surface of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> particle and the interior of the particle was sodium-rich and exterior was sodium-deficient. Therefore, concentration polarization occurred during applied current and slowly disappeared under OCV conditions. This time-dependent cell-voltage relaxation process can be used for obtaining relaxation time. The cells had the magnitude of depolarization voltages of approximately 50–70 mV (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>). This depolarization cell voltage was fitted with <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>. Details about the equation and data analysis were discussed in the reported literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 30 and 33 to 37">(30,33−37)</a> The obtained relaxation time from fitting data was used to derive the sodium ionic diffusivity. Note that the diffusion length was considered to be half of the particle diameter from the SEM micrograph.</div><div class="NLM_p">The extracted relation time and sodium-ion diffusivity for the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d, respectively.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><i>A</i> is the material-dependent constant term and its value changes with changing the ionic and electronic conductivity for a material and <i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>)</sub> and <i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>=∞)</sub> are the depolarized cell voltages at times (<i>t</i>) and <i>t</i> = ∞, respectively. The relaxation time τ can be obtained from the plot of ln(<i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>)</sub> – <i>U</i><sub>(<i>t</i>=∞)</sub>) versus <i>t</i>, which provides ionic diffusivity via <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i14"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The relaxation time followed by sodium-ion diffusivity in Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F showed very complex behavior as a function of sodium concentration. At <i>x</i> = 0.0–0.5, the ionic diffusivity was almost constant and thereafter decreased gradually up to <i>x</i> = 1.0; beyond <i>x</i> = 1.0, ionic diffusivity started to increase further with gradual removal of sodium. The authors could not clearly understand the complex behavior yet. However, the diffusion mechanism and surrounding interaction of the sodium ion might change with the removal of sodium from Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F. Notably, the ionic diffusivity of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F decreased with the removal of sodium as long as the interstitial diffusion mechanism is active. On the other hand, ionic diffusivity increased with the removal of sodium as long as the vacancy diffusion mechanism is active.</div><div class="NLM_p">The obtained sodium-ion diffusivity was around <i>D</i> = ∼10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and did not change considerably with the change in sodium in Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> except at <i>x</i> = 1. To the authors’ knowledge, no diffusivity data are available for this material.</div><div class="NLM_p">It is seen from the <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> that the electrochemical rate performances of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> were limited by activation energy of interfacial charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>3</sub>) at the lower temperature interval and at higher temperature interval, rate performance might be limited by activation energy of ionic diffusivity.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Evolution of interfacial resistances and (b) ionic diffusivity of Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> at <i>x</i> = 1 measured as a function of inverse temperature to obtain activation energy.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Activation energy is a model parameter which is not known for the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> material. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a> shows the charge/discharge profile of Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> from the 0 to 50 °C temperature range to check the compatibility of its electrochemical performance under extreme conditions. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows that the cell capacity at a lower temperature interval was not promising and the cell resistance increased significantly with decreasing temperature. This behavior is another indication that electrochemical performance was limited by interfacial kinetics. To reveal this behavior, the authors measured the impedance spectra at a sodium concentration of <i>x</i> = 1 at different temperatures. The impedance spectra were similar, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a> (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>), and the cell-voltage relaxation at selected temperatures is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>. The same charging and relaxation protocols were followed as discussed previously.</div><div class="NLM_p">The ionic diffusivity and interfacial resistances are plotted as a function of inverse temperature in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b, respectively. The obtained different activation energies are compared in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">3</a>. The activation energy due to sodium-ion diffusion is 0.55 eV and the activation energy of ionic diffusion for Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> has not been reported. However, the activation energy of the ionic conduction of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> has been reported to be 0.68 eV, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> which is consistent with the obtained value. The activation energy due to the ionic conductivity of the electrolyte solution (<i>R</i><sub>1</sub>) and charge-transfer resistance at the Na/electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>2</sub>) and Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interfaces (<i>R</i><sub>3</sub>) was 0.22, 0.26, and 0.33 eV (0.92 eV at lower temperature), respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a shows that <i>R</i><sub>3</sub> exhibited two slopes in the measured temperature window. The charge particles (electron and hole) and temperature interval hindered interfacial kinetics at the Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/electrolyte interfaces.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. Comparison of Activation Energy of Different Resistance Components and Ionic Diffusivity</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">resistance components</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">activation energy (eV)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><i>R</i><sub>1</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.22</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><i>R</i><sub>2</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.26</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><i>R</i><sub>3</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.33 (0.92 at lower <i>T</i>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">ionic diffusion</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.55</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16"> Measurements of Heat Generation</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> displays the voltage and heat generation profiles of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>/Na half-cell during two first charge/discharge cycles at a 0.1 C rate. A good reproducibility was indicated except a slight decrease in the charging and discharging time on the second cycle. The voltage curve exhibits two plateaus in the charge and discharge cycle, which are correlated to anodic and cathodic peaks in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. The heat generation increases from negative to positive values along the low-voltage plateau during charge and then an exothermic peak and an endothermic peak were observed in the middle of charge.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Voltage and heat generation profiles of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> half-cell as a function of cycling time at 0.1 C rate and <i>T</i> = 25 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">These peaks were caused by a phase transition in the cathode, followed by continuous increase in heat. A similar behavior was observed during discharge. This overall heat generation included the contribution of both reversible heat owing to entropic change in the electrochemical reaction and irreversible heat due to cell polarization corresponding to Ohmic losses in the cell, charge transfer at the interfaces, and mass transfer limitations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 39 to 44">(39−44)</a> At the end of each discharge, the heat generation increased sharply up to 7.6 mW/g, which was mainly due to the dominance of the irreversible heat over the reaction heat. Polarization of the electrode significantly increased the overpotential and Ohmic resistance, which was also indicated by the GITT measurements.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a> shows the voltage and heat generation of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode at 0.1 C, 0.2 C, and 1.0 C rates as a function of the SOCs for the charging process and SODs for the discharging process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a shows that the voltage curves for cells tested at 0.1 C and 0.2 C rates were nearly collapsed onto the same curves when plotted against SOC and SOD, respectively. However, the voltage curves for cells tested at the 1.0 C rate appeared to be higher during the charge and lower during the discharge because of the overpotential increase at a high cycling rate. During both charge and discharge processes, the heat generation increased with increasing current rate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b). Endothermic and exothermic heat generation were observed at low and moderate current rates (0.1 C and 0.2 C) because both reversible and irreversible heats contribute to total heat generation at low and moderate current rates. However, only exothermic heat was observed at the high current rate (1.0 C) because usually the irreversible heat dominates the heat generation at a high current rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">4</a> summarizes the heat accumulation data of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode during charge/discharge at different C rates. The accumulated heat was calculated by integrating the heat curves in charge and discharge processes. The amount of heat was increased with the charge/discharge rate. The heat evolution during the discharge process enhanced by 27% from 0.1 C to 0.2 C rate. However, at the 1.0 C rate, the generated heat increased by 89%, which was mostly because of the dominance of the irreversible heat over the reversible heat because of the huge enhancement of the overpotential caused by the rise of the Ohmic resistance and also electrode polarization.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Voltage and (b) heat profiles of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> half-cell cycled at different charge/discharge rates (0.1 C, 0.2 C, and 0.1 C) and <i>T</i> = 25 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl4"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 4. Accumulated Heat of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> Cathode During Charge/Discharge at Different C Rates and <i>T</i> = 25 °C</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">charge/discharge rate (C)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">accumulated heat during charge (mJ)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">accumulated heat during discharge (mJ)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">0.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">158</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">297</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">0.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">244</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">378</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">1.0</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">424</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">542</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">A comparison of the voltage and the heat generation of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> half-cell cycled at a 0.2 C current rate for 25 and 45 °C is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a,b. The voltage profiles showed remarkably measurable variation at both charge and discharge. The largest voltage deviation appeared between 75 and 95% SOD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) likely because of a capacity fade at high temperature.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/medium/am0c11616_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0010.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) Voltage and (b) heat generation profiles of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> half-cell cycled at a 0.2 C rate for <i>T</i> = 25 °C and <i>T</i> = 45 °C and (c) voltage and (d) heat generation profiles of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> half-cell cycled at a 1.0 C rate for <i>T</i> = 25 °C and <i>T</i> = 45 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/asset/images/large/am0c11616_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c11616&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The results in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">4</a> show that the charge and discharge capacities corresponding with the same rate decreased with the increase in the temperature. Additionally, the heat generation in 65–100% SOC was quite high for the cell tested at 45 °C. Also, the heat generation increased significantly with the temperature during the discharge process.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>c,d presents a comparison of voltage and heat generation for the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode cycled under a 1.0 C rate. The voltage profiles during charge and discharge showed differences because of a significant decrease in capacities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>c and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl5" href="#tbl5">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl5" href="#tbl5">5</a>). Comparing results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>d, the heat generation during charge and discharge processes clearly increased significantly with increasing temperature, which is mainly caused by the irreversible heat increase at the high rate and temperature. With the increase in the charge/discharge rates, the effect of temperature also became important because of the dominance of the irreversible component over the reversible component.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl5"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 5. Charge/Discharge Capacity of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> Half-Cell at Two Different Temperatures and Discharge Rates</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" scope="col" align="center">rates</th><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="2" scope="col" align="center" char=".">0.2&nbsp;C</th><th class="rowsep1 colsep0" colspan="2" scope="col" align="center" char=".">1.0&nbsp;C</th></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">temperature (°C)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">charge capacity (mA&nbsp;h/g)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">discharge capacity (mA&nbsp;h/g)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">charge capacity (mA&nbsp;h/g)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">discharge capacity (mA&nbsp;h/g)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">25</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">130</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">128</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">123</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">118</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">45</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">124</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">115</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">119</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">110</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i19">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A new iron-doped sodium vanadium oxyflurophosphate Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> cathode material was synthesized by the hydrothermal method, and its electrochemical performances were examined in half- and full-cell configurations. The electrochemical half-cell performances were examined using three different electrolyte formulations: DMC–EC–PC, DEC–EC, and EC–PC, which exhibited excellent stability at room temperature. The capacity loss was around 15% and the Coulombic efficiency was 99% after 100 cycles with DMC–EC–PC. The Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> versus NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>MWCNT full cell delivered an excellent initial discharge capacity of 80 mA h/g. EIS measurements revealed that charge-transfer kinetics played a major role for the electrochemical rate performances of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub>. The sodium-ion diffusivity was around 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and did not change significantly with changed sodium content (SOC). An electrochemical isothermal calorimetry measurement was also performed to understand the effects of the charge/discharge rate and temperature on heat generation behavior of the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>V<sub>1.7</sub>O(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>2</sub> half-cell. Joule heat and reaction heat were the main heat sources, which depended largely on the operating conditions, including SOC/SOD, charge/discharge rate, and cycling temperature. The generated heat increased at high charge/discharge rates and high temperatures because of the dominance of irreversible heat at high C rates and high temperatures. Based on the experimental results, the authors conclude that the choice of the appropriate operating charge/discharge rate and cycling temperature will affect the thermal stability of this electrode, which may play a key role in overall sodium-ion cell safety. Overall, this work provides a broad framework for the development of novel cathodes toward the development of next-generation NaIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i20">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c11616?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c11616</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional information pertaining to analysis and electrochemical performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c11616/suppl_file/am0c11616_si_001.pdf">am0c11616_si_001.pdf (982.69 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c11616" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">α-Na2Ni2Fe(PO4)3: a dual positive/negative electrode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Essehli, R.; Belharouak, I.; Ben Yahia, H.; Chamoun, R.; Orayech, B.; El Bali, B.; Bouziane, K.; Zhou, X. L.; Zhou, Z.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Dalton Transactions</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4526-4532</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">DTARAF</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1477-9226</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new orthophosphate α-Na2Ni2Fe(PO4)3 was synthesized using a solid state reaction route, and its crystal structure was detd. from powder x-ray diffraction data.  The phys. properties of α-Na2Ni2Fe(PO4)3 were studied by magnetic and electrochem. measurements and by Moessbauer and Raman spectroscopy.  α-Na2Ni2Fe(PO4)3 crystallizes according to a stuffed α-CrPO4-type structure with the space group Imma and the cell parameters a = 10.42821(12), b = 13.19862(15), c = 6.47634(8) Å, and Z = 4.  The structure consists of a three-dimensional framework of octahedra and tetrahedra sharing corners and/or edges with channels along [100] and [010], in which the sodium atoms are located.  The 57Fe Moessbauer spectrum indicates that the Fe3+ cation is distributed over two crystallog. sites implying the presence of a Ni2+/Fe3+ statistical disorder.  Magnetic susceptibility follows the Curie-Weiss behavior above 100 K with θ = -114.3 K indicating the occurrence of predominant antiferromagnetic interactions.  Electrochem. tests indicate that during the first discharge to 1 V vs. Na+/Na in a sodium cell, one Na+ ion could be inserted into the α-Na2Ni2Fe(PO4)3 structure.  This has led to the formation of a new phase Na3Ni2Fe(PO4)3 which was found to be promising as a cathode material for sodium batteries.  When α-Na2Ni2Fe(PO4)3 is further discharged to 0.03 V, it delivers a capacity of 960 mA-h/g.  This corresponds to the intercalation of more than seven sodium atoms per formula unit which is an indication of a conversion-type behavior with the formation of metallic Fe and Ni.  When cycled in the voltage range 0.03-3 V vs. Na+/Na, at 20°, under the current rates of 50, 100, 200, and 400 mA/g, reversible capacities of 238, 196, 153, and 115 mA-h/g, resp., were obtained.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpw21ByM-sln7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li58qffuj8EKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVGiurY%253D&amp;md5=b941c2c9975f775eb2a129304216a4ad</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c5dt00971e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Essehli, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Belharouak, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ben Yahia, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maher, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abouimrane, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Orayech, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Calder, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Alluaudite Na2Co2Fe(PO4)3 as an electroactive material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Essehli, R.; Belharouak, I.; Ben Yahia, H.; Maher, K.; Abouimrane, A.; Orayech, B.; Calder, S.; Zhou, X. L.; Zhou, Z.; Sun, Y-K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Dalton Transactions</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7881-7886</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">DTARAF</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1477-9226</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electroactive orthophosphate Na2Co2Fe(PO4)3 was synthesized using a solid state reaction.  Its crystal structure was solved using the combination of powder X-ray- and neutron-diffraction data.  This material crystallizes according to the alluaudite structure (S.G. C2/c).  The structure consists of edge sharing [MO6] octahedra (M = Fe, Co) resulting in chains parallel to [-101].  These chains are linked together via the [PO4] tetrahedra to form two distinct tunnels in which sodium cations are located.  The electrochem. properties of Na2Co2Fe(PO4)3 were evaluated by galvanostatic charge-discharge cycling.  During the first discharge to 0.03 V, Na2Co2Fe(PO4)3 delivers a specific capacity of 604 mA h g-1.  This capacity is equiv. to the reaction of &gt;7 sodium ions per formula unit.  Hence, this is a strong indication of a conversion-type reaction with the formation of metallic Fe and Co.  The subsequent charge and discharge involved the reaction of fewer Na ions as expected for a conversion reaction.  When discharged to 0.9 V, the material intercalated only one Na+-ion leading to the formation of a new phase Na3Co2Fe(PO4)3.  This phase could then be cycled reversibly with an av. voltage of 3.6 V vs. Na+/Na and a capacity of 110 mA h g-1.  This result is in good agreement with the theor. capacity expected from the extn./insertion of two sodium atoms in Na3Co2Fe(PO4)3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqUKJ4AKPdoU7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liWu_j4JvG0_w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXkvFGitL8%253D&amp;md5=f67235f5f9a8c7889e4874a1081c9f10</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2016.05.125"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Essehli, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ben Yahia, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maher, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sougrati, M. T.; Abouimrane, A.; Park, J.-B.; Sun, Y.-K.; Al-Maadeed, M. A.; Belharouak, I.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

Ramesh; Kahraman, Ramazan; Al-Qaradawi, Siham; Soliman, Ahmed</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na4MnV(PO4)3 is a Na ion conducting material with a NASICON type crystal structure.  This phase is not much known as an electrode material.  The present work focuses on the Na ion intercalation/de-intercalation mechanism and charge/discharge behavior of the material.  The Na4MnV(PO4)3 was synthesized through a sol-gel process and characterized by XRD, SEM, and XPS.  The structural anal. confirms the formation of a phase pure cryst. material with nanometric particle size which adopts a trigonal crystal structure.  Galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT) measurements indicate that Na4MnV(PO4)3 is electrochem. active having slanting voltage plateaus.  Ex-situ and In-situ XRD anal., as a function of Na concn., indicate that the intercalation/de-intercalation of Na is assocd. with a single-phase reaction rather than a biphasic reaction when cycled between 1.5 and 4.5 V.  The electrochem. measurements on composite electrodes, Na4MnV(PO4)3/CNTS (1 and 3%), show promising charge/discharge capacity (∼140 mAh/g), good cyclability (100% capacity retention after 40 cycles) and reasonable rate capability.  The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and XPS analyses indicate that the main contributions towards the activity of Na4MnV(PO4)3 can be attributed to the active of Mn2+/Mn3+ and V3+/V4+ redox couple with partial activity of V4+/V5+.  The obtained results suggest that Na4MnV(PO4)3 is a promising electrode material which can be achieved better rate performance with long cycling stability and battery performance through engineering of the particle morphol. and microstructure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrU5lBfWFEHErVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0aOK6h6P-jA"> &gt;&gt; Here we report a sodium super-ionic conductor structured electrode, sodium vanadium titanium phosphate, which delivers a high specific capacity of 147 mA h g-1 at a rate of 0.1 C and excellent capacity retentions at high rates.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anode-Free Sodium Battery through in Situ Plating of Sodium Metal</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cohn, Adam P.; Muralidharan, Nitin; Carter, Rachel; Share, Keith; Pint, Cary L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been pursued as a more cost-effective and more sustainable alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), but these advantages come at the expense of energy d.  In this work, we demonstrate that the challenge of energy d. for sodium chemistries can be overcome through an anode-free architecture enabled by the use of a nanocarbon nucleation layer formed on Al current collectors.  Electrochem. studies show this configuration to provide highly stable and efficient plating and stripping of sodium metal over a range of currents up to 4 mA/cm2, sodium loading up to 12 mAh/cm2, and with long-term durability exceeding 1000 cycles at a current of 0.5 mA/cm2. (387 Wh/kg for LiCoO2/graphite cells) while still relying on naturally abundant raw materials and cost-effective aq. processing.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrvDyk7myMZMrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liRfo_eEbYNrw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtlajt7o%253D&amp;md5=a2e9af7af17709d65613ed0facebff2a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c8ta05911j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cohn, A. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">23875</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">23884</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rethinking sodium-ion anodes as nucleation layers for anode-free batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cohn, Adam P.; Metke, Thomas; Donohue, Jennifer; Muralidharan, Nitin; Share, Keith; Pint, Cary L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">23875-23884</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we report a room-temp. sodium metal battery, where the sodium initially stored in a Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode is plated, upon charging, onto an aluminum current collector coated with a thin nucleation layer.  To maximize the battery performance, conventional sodium-ion anode materials, including non-graphitized carbons and sodium-alloying metals, were evaluated as nucleation layers to facilitate stable electroplating of sodium metal.  Among several materials studied, carbon black and bismuth showed the highest sodium plating-stripping coulombic efficiencies in half-cell testing, averaging 99.9% and 99.85%, resp., over 50 cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  Building on these findings, anode-free cells with Na3V2(PO4)3 cathodes were assembled in a discharged state, demonstrating energy densities up to 318 W h kg-1 at 0.25 mA cm-2 (∼C/6), a first-cycle coulombic efficiency up to 92%, a stable discharge voltage at 3.35 V, an av. round-trip energy efficiency of 98%, and a capacity retention of 82.5% after 100 cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2 (∼C/3).  With its unique performance highlighted in this work, the anode-free sodium battery emerges as a low-cost, high-performance option for stationary elec. storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpZqUt5HNQ_97Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liRfo_eEbYNrw"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Polyanionic materials such as Na3V2(PO4)2F3-yOy (0 ≤ y ≤ 2) are of high interest as pos. electrode for Na-ion batteries since they offer competitive electrochem. performances compared to sodiated transition metal oxides.  The compn. Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (y = 0) has the highest theor. energy d. among the series, but surprisingly a lot of discrepancies are reported throughout the literature considering its structure and its electrochem. properties.  We will show that most of the compds. reported as being Na3VIII2(PO4)2F3 are in fact slightly oxidized due to synthesis conditions resulting in a partial oxygen substitution for fluorine.  To get an in-depth understanding of this system, a series of compns. Na3V2(PO4)2F3-yOy (0 ≤ y ≤ 0.5, i.e., near the fluorine-rich compn.) was synthesized and characterized combining synchrotron X-ray diffraction, X-ray absorption spectroscopy, solid state NMR spectroscopy, and galvanostatic electrochem. tests.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">β-NaVOPO4 Obtained by a Low-Temperature Synthesis Process: A New 3.3 V Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Guang; Huq, Ashfia; Kan, Wang Hay; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Vanadyl phosphates (VOPO4) represent a class of attractive cathodes in Li-ion batteries.  However, the exploration of this type of materials in Na-ion batteries is rare.  Here, the authors report for the 1st time the synthesis of orthorhombic β-NaVOPO4 by 1st chem. extg. Li from β-LiVOPO4 and then inserting Na into the obtained β-VOPO4 by a microwave-assisted solvothermal process with NaI, which serves both as a reducing agent and Na source.  Intermediate NaxVOPO4 compns. with x = 0.3, 0.5, and 0.8 have also been obtained by controlling the amt. of NaI in the reaction mixt.  Joint Rietveld refinement of synchrotron XRD and n diffraction confirms that the fully sodiated β-NaVOPO4 is isostructural with the Li counterpart β-LiVOPO4.  Bond valence sum maps suggest that Na ions possibly diffuse along the [010] direction in the lattice, similar to the ionic conduction pathway in β-LiVOPO4.  Although the initial discharge capacity is low due to the protons in the structure, it steadily increases with cycling with a long plateau at 3.3 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To solve the problem of poor cond., monoclinic NaVPO4F with a uniform carbon coating layer is synthesized via a facile soln. approach with assistance of polyvinyl pyrrolidone (PVP). On the other hand, PVP is used as carbon source, in-situ generating a uniform carbon coating layer on the surface of NaVPO4F particles.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A vanadium oxy-phosphate Na4VO(PO4)2 as cathode material for Na ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Deriouche, W.; Anger, E.; Freire, M.; Maignan, A.; Amdouni, N.; Pralong, V.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Sciences</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Na4VO(PO4)2 phase has been synthesized using a solid state route in sealed tubes.  This phase crystg. in the orthorhombic system has a one-dimensional structure built up of corner shared VO6 and PO4 polyhedra.  It is an ionic conductor with a cond. of 10-4S/cm at 500 K and an activation energy of 0.63eV.  The electrochem. properties of Na4VO(PO4)2 as a pos. electrode in a sodium ion battery have been studied.  Results show the possibility of extg. almost one sodium through a two-phase process at 3.4 V leading to the phase Na3.2VO(PO4)2 and to insert as well one sodium at low potential 1.5 V, to form through a solid soln. process the reduced phase Na5VO(PO4)2.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">1D Nanostructured Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) as High-Potential and Superior-Performance Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Deng, Chao; Zhang, Sen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tailoring materials into nanostructure offers unprecedented opportunities in the utilization of their functional properties.  High-purity Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) with 1D nanostructure is prepd. as a cathode material for rechargeable Na-ion batteries.  An efficient synthetic approach is developed by carefully controlling the crystal growth in the molten sodium phosphate.  Based on the XRD, XPS, TG, and morphol. characterization, a molten-salt assisted mechanism for nanoarchitecture formation is revealed.  The prepd. Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) nanorod has rectangle sides and preferential [001] growth orientation.  Galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique evaluation indicates that the sodium de/intercalation of Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) nanorod involves V3+/V4+ redox reaction and Na5V3.5+4(P2O7)4(PO4) as intermediate phase, which results in two pairs of potential plateaus at the equil. potentials of 3.8713 V (V3+/V3.5+) and 3.8879 V (V3.5+/V4+), resp.  The unique nanoarchitecture of the phase-pure Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) facilitates its reversible sodium de/intercalation, which is beneficial to the high-rate capability and the cycling stability.  The Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) cathode delivers 80% of the capacity (obtained at C/20) at the 10 C rate and 95% of the initial capacity after 200 cycles.  Therefore, it is feasible to design and fabricate an advanced rechargeable sodium-ion battery by employment of 1D nanostructured Na7V4(P2O7)4(PO4) as the cathode material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo4Qd2_IQczjbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgcGNAqCSUHUA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXos1Wltrw%253D&amp;md5=8f8ecee270b2407608b9eef53772a252</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201912964"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cortie, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, a 4.0 V class high-voltage cathode material is introduced with a newly recognized sodium superionic conductor (NASICON)-type structure with cubic symmetry (space group P213), Na3V(PO3)3N.  A N-doped graphene oxide-wrapped Na3V(PO3)3N composite is synthesized with a uniform carbon coating layer, which shows excellent rate performance and outstanding cycling stability. Its 3D sodium diffusion pathways were demonstrated with distinctive low energy barriers.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na3V2O2(PO4)2F/graphene sandwich structure for high-performance cathode of a sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Maowen; Wang, Long; Zhao, Xin; Song, Jie; Xie, Hui; Lu, Yuhao; Goodenough, John B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
